Burning was the extreme punishment for an Egyptian because it destroyed the body and with it the hope of an afterlife: the victim ceased to exist."[823]
♠ purity ♣ present ♥ In Egypt, purity (‘’wab’’) is the absence of ‘’bwt’’, i.e. any manifestation of evil, “whether this-worldly or otherworldly, social or cosmological’ [824]
♠ kinship ♣ present ♥ “Egyptian instructional literature advises the individual to have fun as a child, then to get an education and find employment, and, finally, to find a spouse and start a family. If the marriage does not work out, the person is advised to get a divorce and find another, more compatible, companion. Among the other admonitions in the Egyptian literature are do not lie or steal, honor your mother and father, care for and respect the aged, and show up for your job (but it is all right to cheat a little on the “time card”). Live a modest life - do not be a braggart, do not start fights, beware of prostitutes (they will “cut your purse”), and be obedient to your superiors.” [825] “Our earliest source for ethical values is autobiographies, attested from the fifth dynasty (c.2510-2374 BCE) onward, and usually addressed to succeeding generations. The official Nefer-seshem-re (c.2350 BCE) says: ‘[...] I respected my father, I pleased my mother,/I nurtured their children.’ (Lichtheim 1973, p. 17) [...] the later teachings (e.g., Amenompe) and autobiographies (e.g., An-hurmose) still espouse the same ethical standards as the earlier sources and are just as interested in social cohesion.” [826]
♠ bravery ♣ present ♥ “Our earliest source for ethical values is autobiographies, attested from the fifth dynasty (c.2510-2374 BCE) onward, and usually addressed to succeeding generations. The official Nefer-seshem-re (c.2350 BCE) says: ‘[...] I rescued the weak from the hand of one stronger than he when I was able. [...]' (Lichtheim 1973, p. 17) [...] the later teachings (e.g., Amenompe) and autobiographies (e.g., An-hurmose) still espouse the same ethical standards as the earlier sources and are just as interested in social cohesion. What changes is the argument in favor of these standards: in the traditional concept it is that they comply with maat and lead to success in this life and the next; now, it is that they are the will of the god. Success in this life is subordinated to one's relationship with the god, but the latter also determines one's fate after death. Thus at the end of Chapter 24, 13-17, Amenompe says, “Happy is he who reaches the hereafter when he is safe in the hand of god.” There, at the final judgment in the “hall of double (complete) maat” where the eternal fate of a person is decided, maat still has a central role.” [827]
♠ respect for property ♣ present ♥ “Egyptian instructional literature advises the individual to have fun as a child, then to get an education and find employment, and, finally, to find a spouse and start a family. If the marriage does not work out, the person is advised to get a divorce and find another, more compatible, companion. Among the other admonitions in the Egyptian literature are do not lie or steal, honor your mother and father, care for and respect the aged, and show up for your job (but it is all right to cheat a little on the “time card”). Live a modest life - do not be a braggart, do not start fights, beware of prostitutes (they will “cut your purse”), and be obedient to your superiors.” [828] “This development was to culminate in chapter 125 of the New Kingdom Book of Going Forth by Day(Lichtheim, 1976, pp. 124-132), which deals with the judgment before the god of the netherworld, Osiris. [830]

♠ Moralistic punishment by human religious agents ♣ inferred absent ♥ This variable is meant to capture whether or not religious agents were able to punish people's moral transgressions independently of the state. That this was not normally the case is inferred from three things. First, the general expectation was that people who acted in an immoral fashion would be punished in the next life, not this one [831]. Second, though there is much that is still not known about Egyptian priestly classes, it seems that the only ones who had any say in matters of moral transgression were oracles, who were called on to ask the Gods to arbitrate disputes and determine whether someone had committed a crime or not [832]: but, once they had done that, no evidence could be found in the literature suggesting that these oracles also punished the criminal. Third, there is ample evidence that criminal prosecution, including for crimes of a religious nature, was in the hands of State officials [833].

Well-Being[edit]

Economic Well-Being[edit]

State Intervention in Economy

♠ The state regulates banking ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not applicable. No banks existed in this period. [853]
♠ Price controls ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Intervention in the form of massive storage capacity creating buffer-stocks was standard in ancient Egypt, too, both at the institutional level and within the economies of private estates whether in times of peace and plenty (e.g. the Amarna estates) or in times of famine and unrest (e.g. Ankhtify). This was a passive intervention which did not extend to official attempts to regulate prices."[854] "As understood today, there is little reason to believe that the various titles borne by officials in Ancient Egypt demonstrate any kind of control of the economy, or even that these official titles represented a significant aspect of the economy of Ancient Egypt."[855]
♠ Regulations against hoarding/stockpiling goods ♣ inferred absent ♥ "Intervention in the form of massive storage capacity creating buffer-stocks was standard in ancient Egypt, too, both at the institutional level and within the economies of private estates whether in times of peace and plenty (e.g. the Amarna estates) or in times of famine and unrest (e.g. Ankhtify). This was a passive intervention which did not extend to official attempts to regulate prices."[856] Overseer of granaries, Overseer of cattle "both concerned with regulating economic life and storing or securing the taxes owed to the state." [857]

♠ Pensions ♣ ♥
♠ Standardization of weights/measures ♣ present ♥ "The basic units of grain were the 'sack' (XAr) and its subdivisions, the hekat (HoAt) and the oipe (ipt). In the New Kingdom, the sack was a unit of almost 80 liters, subdivided into four oipe, each of which in its turn was made up of four hekat. A further subdivision, the hin (hnw) (1/10 of the hekat, approximately 1/2 of a liter), was used for fluids, but not for grain (Reineke 1963)."[858]
♠ Guarantee stability of coin value ♣ absent ♥ No coinage existed at this time.
♠ Public markets ♣ inferred present ♥ implying government initiatives were more typical? - "A trade station operated at the oasis of Dakhla from 1700 BCE on, apparently separate from any state initiative (Marchand and Soukiassian 2010), while Libyan peoples traded with Levantine sailors around Mersa Matruh in New Kingdom times (White 2002: 47-53 and 168-74)."[859] New Kingdom state "participated extensively in markets - in fact had some of its largest impacts on ordinary Egyptians' daily lives through routine market activities." [860] "manufacturers, whose titles affiliate them with the Egyptian state, retail their products on the open market to other representatives of the state."[861]

Patronage

♠ within-class formal patronage ♣ inferred present ♥ "self-sufficiency was an ideal hardly attainable for many Egyptians, who were thus forced to borrow from richer neighbors, to work (at least part-time or seasonally) for institutions and wealthier neighbors, or to enter into patronage networks that perpetuated social inequalities between households."[862] "Gift-giving was another important relationship; the kings regularly rewarded deserving officials in public ceremonies and, in turn, received New Year gifts from officials and institutions."[863] "kings returning from campaigns offered part of the booty to the gods and many endowed temples with large areas of land."[864] New Kingdom: "The bonds of some prominent families with the royal family thus appear clearly, with women being sent to the harem as wives or concubines, while their male relatives occupied prominent positions in the palace and in the administration (KRI V 350-366)."[865] Officials could also be recruited from the provincial administration, and royal buildings constructed, in the locality whence the royal wife or concubine came[866] - activities which provide employment, raise status of people associated with the woman.
♠ between-class formal patronage ♣ ♥ "self-sufficiency was an ideal hardly attainable for many Egyptians, who were thus forced to borrow from richer neighbors, to work (at least part-time or seasonally) for institutions and wealthier neighbors, or to enter into patronage networks that perpetuated social inequalities between households."[867] New Kingdom patronage networks controlled by local potentates.[868]
♠ within-class informal patronage ♣ inferred present ♥ "Patronage, informal networks of influence, factions, corruption, and favoritism 'oiled' the everyday functioning of power in pre-industrial states".[869] "self-sufficiency was an ideal hardly attainable for many Egyptians, who were thus forced to borrow from richer neighbors, to work (at least part-time or seasonally) for institutions and wealthier neighbors, or to enter into patronage networks that perpetuated social inequalities between households."[870] "Gift-giving was another important relationship; the kings regularly rewarded deserving officials in public ceremonies and, in turn, received New Year gifts from officials and institutions."[871] "kings returning from campaigns offered part of the booty to the gods and many endowed temples with large areas of land."[872] "the ideal of self-sufficiency proclaimed in many private inscriptions was hardly achievable for many Egyptians, who were obliged to depend on powerful or influential fellow citizens and to join their patronage networks, to the point of being considered part of their households."[873] general reference

♠ provision of food storage ♣ present ♥ "Egyptian households should not be considered limited to nuclear families as they were frequently multifaceted social networks embracing other relatives, serfs, clients, subordinates, and dependants, especially at the uppermost levels of Pharaonic society. Thus, the silos in the richest villas of el-Amarna could be interpreted both as indicators of status and as the foci of a redistributive system involving not only their owners but also their relatives and dependants, also considered members of the household (Kemp 1991: 309 - 310)."[881]

Periodic reforms of abuses are well documented, and officials' biographies frequently refer to their aid to the disadvantaged."[882] Famine and poor relief "standard claims of elites in most periods."[883]

[889] Shaduf introduced middle second millennium BCE [890], which would be around start of the New Kingdom.

Entertainment and Knowledge Well-Being[edit]

♠ Funding for festivals ♣ present ♥ "sed-festival, the jubilee that renewed kingship"[897] Opet festival, annual event at Luxor from Queen Hatshepsut's reign, which initially lasted 11 days but had become extended to 27 days by the end of the New Kingdom.[898]

♠ Entertainment buildings ♣ inferred present ♥ "Music was performed in several types of spaces, public and private: inside the temple (fig. 2), the palace, during religious processions (fig. 16), military parades (fig. 10), during burials to maintain the funerary cult, or also during private festivities (fig. 1)."[899] "Westerners who find anomalous the custom of prostitution in a sacred place should appreciate that the temple was not merely, or even primarily, a place of worship, but was the economic, cultural, and social center of the village.... Generally, the temple was a predominant land-holder, usurer, employer, school, and "concert-hall" as well as brothel ... (Temples in ancient Egypt, Greece, and Babylon may have functioned similarly)."[900]

♠ Theatres ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned as a space for musical performance by Emeritt.[901] Acting does not appear to have been a profession at this time: "Egyptian documents that show the best evidence for performances of dramatic characters are almost all connected with or part of ritual routines."[902] However, while there is some evidence "that narrative-based performances with interaction between characters existed" specialised theatres for acting would seem unlikely as one might expect either archaeological or documentary evidence for them as we have for ancient Greece.
♠ Arenas ♣ inferred absent ♥ Not mentioned as a space for musical performance by Emeritt.[903] Acting does not appear to have been a profession at this time: "Egyptian documents that show the best evidence for performances of dramatic characters are almost all connected with or part of ritual routines."[904] However, while there is some evidence "that narrative-based performances with interaction between characters existed" specialised arenas for acting would seem unlikely as one might expect either archaeological or documentary evidence for them as we have for ancient Greece.

[907] Professional musicians and choirs.[908] "Music was performed in several types of spaces, public and private: inside the temple (fig. 2), the palace, during religious processions (fig. 16), military parades (fig. 10), during burials to maintain the funerary cult, or also during private festivities (fig. 1)."[909]

♠ status of full-time entertainment specialists ♣ equal ♥ "Musicians’ titles also indicate that they often occupied other functions in Egyptian society. It was usually a position in the priestly hierarchy, but they could also attain offices in the royal administration."[910] "the male musicians’ social recognition seems superior to that of the female musicians because some male musicians possess their own tomb, which is a sign of royal favor. Thus, this privilege was not restricted to persons in charge of religious, administrative, political, or military tasks. The economic and social status of female musicians (Hsyt, šmayt, and jHyt) seems to rather be determined by that of their spouse, especially from the New Kingdom onwards (Fantechi and Zingarelli 2002: 32). Often married to a high dignitary, they are represented or named with him on their monuments (be this tombs, stelae, or statues)."[911]

♠ Access to education ♣ present ♥ "Basic education offered in Egyptian local schools consisted of the teaching of language, mathematics, geography, and of other subjects appropriate for the preparation of potential scribes who were destined to work in local and national Egyptian institutions, such as the palace or the temples."[912] Ezzamel (2002) does not specifically mention schools, or local schools, for the New Kingdom but his reference to "wise elderly men"[913] could imply the existence of local schools. Many scribes were educated by their fathers or "wise elderly men". A few received education from "special instructors in the Pharaoh's palace. They were taught reading, writing and arithmetic."[914]

♠ Publicly funded or subsidized education ♣ inferred present ♥ A few, likely recruited from within the elite, were instructed within the Pharoah's palace, which would have been at the cost to the state. "Basic education offered in Egyptian local schools consisted of the teaching of language, mathematics, geography, and of other subjects appropriate for the preparation of potential scribes who were destined to work in local and national Egyptian institutions, such as the palace or the temples."[915] Ezzamel (2002) does not specifically mention schools, or local schools, for the New Kingdom but his reference to "wise elderly men"[916] could imply the existence of local schools. Many scribes were educated by their fathers or "wise elderly men". A few received education from "special instructors in the Pharaoh's palace. They were taught reading, writing and arithmetic."[917]
♠ Schools for children? ♣ present ♥ "Basic education offered in Egyptian local schools consisted of the teaching of language, mathematics, geography, and of other subjects appropriate for the preparation of potential scribes who were destined to work in local and national Egyptian institutions, such as the palace or the temples."[918] Ezzamel (2002) does not specifically mention schools, or local schools, for the New Kingdom but his reference to "wise elderly men"[919] could imply the existence of local schools. Many scribes were educated by their fathers or "wise elderly men". A few received education from "special instructors in the Pharaoh's palace. They were taught reading, writing and arithmetic."[920]
♠ Universities, “proto-universities”, or other higher education ♣ inferred absent ♥ "ancient Egyptian education prepared the young members of middle and upper classes for entering the labor force of the country and actively participating in various professions and duties related to the civil, priestly, and military spheres. However, unlike education nowadays, the Egyptian school system mainly offered basic and rarely advanced training. Hence, there was probably no Egyptian equivalent to a modern university with its broad educational horizons and its large diversity of specializations."[921]
♠ Libraries ♣ present ♥ "The meaning of pr-anx is still debated, since scholarly circles are divided between its translation as "school" and as "scriptorium" (that is, the space in which scribes worked— studying, producing, and copying various texts) or "university" (in the sense of an institute of advanced learning in contrast to the basic teaching offered in a common school). The pr-anx in the sense of a scriptorium is closely associated with the function of the Egyptian temple, given that most of the major temples included libraries and archives that were probably managed and maintained by scribes, the primary product of school education (for the existence of temple libraries and archives, see Burkard 1980; Quirke 1996; Zinn 2007)."[922]
♠ Museums ♣ inferred absent ♥
♠ Literacy rate ♣ [15-30] ♥ "it seems that school education was primarily for the elite and mostly for the male members of the Egyptian society, who were destined to work for the main Egyptian socio-political and religious institutions, such as the palace, the treasury, or the temple."[923] "ancient Egyptian education prepared the young members of middle and upper classes for entering the labor force of the country and actively participating in various professions and duties related to the civil, priestly, and military spheres."[924] Material uncovered at Deir el-Media "indicates how important the use of writing became in society at large" although this worker-village was likely more literate than the average community in New Kingdom Egypt.[925]

Disease

♠ Endemic disease ♣ ♥ Tombos was "one of the very few sites in Nubia that was continuously occupied from the New Kingdom through the Napatan period" (660 BCE). The New Kingdom "individuals at Tombos showed biological affinities with both Egyptians and Nubians". "The transition from Egyptian to local Napatan rule had few deleterious effects on the community at Tombos in terms of indications of nutritional deficiencies and infectious disease. ... It is clear that the community at Tombos continued to thrive."[926]

Economy variables (polity-level)[edit]

State Income[edit]

♠ Tribute ♣ present ♥ inw and hsb.t "It may, in fact, be reasonably presumed that tribute, booty and foreign trade declined after Ramesses III ..."[934] "It was in contact with distant cultures from which it received exotic gifts."[935] "Gardiner translates hsb.t as dues, and inw as tribute, but the latter word has also been translated as official gifts (Bleiberg, 1996), while neutrally it refers to additions (to revenue or produce). Despite the different meanings, all these words seem to refer in one sense or another to collection of revenues for the state, but inw appears to be more frequently used in the context of gifts."[936] yn-w tribute on foreign subjects, whom could also be levied hsb.t.[937]

Taxation[edit]

♠ Degree of taxation ♣ ♥ "Basically, every institution had two types of agrarian domains. In the terms of Gardiner (1948), these were: “non-apportioning” (presumably worked or supervised by the institution’s own personnel); and “apportioning,” or p(s)S (cultivated by other institutions or private individuals). The major part of the crops of apportioning fields was kept by the parties taking care of their cultivation, while a small part (varying between 7.5% and 15%) went to what Gardiner considered to be the owning institution. This institution, however, should rather be considered not as the “owner” but as having been entitled to tax received from the land (the percentage specified): apportioning fields were often in the hands of private individuals, who were the actual owners, and who yearly paid tax to the temple or government institution (Haring 1998). ... The people cultivating their own land and paying their tax to the royal treasury are there called nmH(y) (plural: nmHyw), a word originally meaning “orphan,” but which in the New Kingdom had acquired the additional meaning “free” or “private,” and referred to people who owned property, but were not among the higher state and temple officials (sr; plural: srw; for this opposition see Romer 1994: 412 - 451)."[941] General reference: "Ancient Egypt imposed taxation in grain, with an overall tax rate of some 10 percent of total production, but higher on some classes of land."[942] General reference: "The harvest tax, probably one of the earliest, was enforced by an official annual survey."[943] "The payment of the state land tax in gold ... suggests an attempt at monetization."[944] General reference: 1/10 (1 h3r per 10 h3r) cite: Baer 1962. 10% yield cite: Bowman 1986.[945] "Levies were not based on how much an acre had produced that year, which would have encouraged farmers to hide part of the crop to lower their tax. Instead, a careful record was kept of how high the Nile rose during its annual inundation using 'nilometers,' stone markets set along the river's course. The height of that year's flood determined what, compared with the previous year, each farm should have produced regardless of what it did produce, and set a tax rate which generally hovered at about 10 percent ... Those who did not hand over their levied amounts were beaten or imprisoned." [946] The practice of estimating tax from capacity in this way is known from the Middle Kingdom and continued into the New Kingdom.[947]


Taxes are imposed on:

♠ Land ♣ present ♥ Land tax.[948] Overseer of granaries, Overseer of cattle "both concerned with regulating economic life and storing or securing the taxes owed to the state." [949] "The vizier functions as a court of appeal on local agricultural affairs and disputes, in casu land-measurement, tax-assessment, the allotment of labourers etc."[950] "Levies were not based on how much an acre had produced that year, which would have encouraged farmers to hide part of the crop to lower their tax. Instead, a careful record was kept of how high the Nile rose during its annual inundation using 'nilometers,' stone markets set along the river's course. The height of that year's flood determined what, compared with the previous year, each farm should have produced regardless of what it did produce, and set a tax rate which generally hovered at about 10 percent ... Those who did not hand over their levied amounts were beaten or imprisoned." [951] Khato-lands were a "taxable subject" during the New Kingdom.[952]
♠ Property ♣ ♥ yp.w "In the ancient Egyptian language, there is no exact equivalent to the modern word 'tax'. ... Gardiner (1941) translates yp.w as dues imposed on officials by virtue of holding a certain office, and h3kw as taxes on ordinary people. More precisely yp.w refers to items counted and b3kw refers to items produced by labour. The word smw refers to harvest-yield, but Gardiner (ibid) prefers harvest-tax. Gardiner translates hsb.t as dues, and inw as tribute, but the latter word has also been translated as official gifts (Bleiberg, 1996), while neutrally it refers to additions (to revenue or produce). Despite the different meanings, all these words seem to refer in one sense or another to collection of revenues for the state, but inw appears to be more frequently used in the context of gifts."[953] Temples were a "taxable subject" during the New Kingdom.[954]
♠ People ♣ ♥ yp.w? Officials and ordinary people were taxable subjects during the New Kingdom.[955] "In the ancient Egyptian language, there is no exact equivalent to the modern word 'tax'. ... Gardiner (1941) translates yp.w as dues imposed on officials by virtue of holding a certain office".[956]
♠ Trade ♣ present ♥ Dues collected from trade. "Apart from the costs of transport itself, there were tolls and customs to be paid. Tolls had to be paid when passing military strongholds in Egypt and Nubia, although temple ships could be exempted by royal decree."[957]
♠ Income ♣ ♥
♠ Production ♣ present ♥ b3kw "In the ancient Egyptian language, there is no exact equivalent to the modern word 'tax'. ... Gardiner (1941) translates yp.w as dues imposed on officials by virtue of holding a certain office, and b3kw as taxes on ordinary people. More precisely yp.w refers to items counted and b3kw refers to items produced by labour. The word smw refers to harvest-yield, but Gardiner (ibid) prefers harvest-tax. Gardiner translates hsb.t as dues, and inw as tribute, but the latter word has also been translated as official gifts (Bleiberg, 1996), while neutrally it refers to additions (to revenue or produce). Despite the different meanings, all these words seem to refer in one sense or another to collection of revenues for the state, but inw appears to be more frequently used in the context of gifts."[958] Funerary model from Middle Kingdom tomb potrays a cattle census "a regular event, whereby the necessary amount of royal tax on livestock would have been calculated."[959] Overseer of granaries, Overseer of cattle "both concerned with regulating economic life and storing or securing the taxes owed to the state." [960] b3kw tax "was extracted from produce, such as wheat, corn, and other foodstuffs such as cattle, fish, and game. It seems that all citizens with a physical/material produce had to pay taxes, unless they were exempt for some obvious reason; which as abolishing tax on fodder gathered for festive and funerary offerings in the Amana period".[961]

Taxes are imposed by:

♠ executive or ruling elite ♣ present ♥ Overseer of granaries, Overseer of cattle "both concerned with regulating economic life and storing or securing the taxes owed to the state." [962]
♠ local elites ♣ present ♥ "the functions of mayors in general were clear; they were responsible for collecting taxes, facilitating the work of representatives of the central government, and implementing orders received from them."[963]
♠ state officials ♣ present ♥ "The civil government was concerned primarily with regulating agriculture, collecting taxes, administering justice and maintaining civic order and its orders were enforced by a relatively weak police force, the Medjayu."[964] According to New Kingdom text "Duties of the Vizier" "The mayors, governors of domains and every subject report their income to him." [965]

Type of taxes paid:

♠ Staple ♣ present ♥ List of items tax paid in for New Kingdom: Gold, rainment, chest of linen, "large bolts", silver, oxen, calves, necklace bead, "1 two-year-old" and "two-year-olds" (two-year-old what?), garments, yearlings, pigeons, firstlings of the year, honey.[966]
♠ Labor ♣ inferred present ♥ "Obligations of the population in terms of taxes, compulsory labour levies and the like are well known..."[967]
♠ Currency ♣ absent ♥


Wages and Costs[edit]

♠ Wages ♣ ♥ "The early 18th dynasty thus initiated a period of new prosperity and granduer for Egypt. ... The wealth generated enabled numerous state departments in the civil, military, and religious branches of government to live in luxury and to expend many resources on their tombs."[968]

In Ancient Egypt "the labour of a mere 150,000 agricultural labourers would have sufficed to nourish a population of several millions."[969] "Egypt was nowhere near full employment, and thus labour will only have had a scarcity value because it may have been very badly remunerated - and not because labour itself was scare. ... My proposition here is thus that these various sources suggest that in ancient Egypt the wages to which free labourers were viewed as being entitled were viewed in social terms and expressed in terms of an economic value or price, rather than being defined or understood solely in terms of production. This labour value was thus not related to the product, but rather to the labour."[970]
